					margin-bottom: 0 !important;
					padding-bottom: 0 !important;
				}.tdi_37 .td_module_wrap .td-module-container:before{
					display: block !important;
				}.tdi_37 .td_module_wrap:nth-last-child(-n+1) .td-module-container:before{
					display: none !important;
				}.tdi_37 .td-module-container:before{
					bottom: -10px;
				}.tdi_37 .td-module-title a{
                    box-shadow: inset 0 0 0 0 #f0a83b;
                }.tdi_37 .td-module-container:hover .td-module-title a{

                        transition: all 0.2s ease;

                    }
                }
}
</style><div class="tdb-block-inner td-fix-index"><div class="tdb-drop-down-search" aria-labelledby="td-header-search-button"><div class="tdb-drop-down-search-inner"><form method="get" class="tdb-search-form" action="https://www.linuxtoday.com/"><div class="tdb-search-form-inner"><input class="tdb-head-search-form-input" placeholder=" " type="text" value="" name="s" autocomplete="off" /><button class="wpb_button wpb_btn-inverse btn tdb-head-search-form-btn" type="submit"><span>Search</span></button></div></form><div class="tdb-aj-search"></div></div></div><a href="#" role="button" aria-label="Search" class="tdb-head-search-btn dropdown-toggle" data-toggle="dropdown"><i class="tdb-search-icon td-icon-search"></i></a></div></div> <!-- ./block --></div></div></div></div></div></div>                    </div>

                            <div class="td_block_wrap tdb_title tdi_56 tdb-single-title td-pb-border-top td_block_template_2" data-td-block-uid="tdi_56">
                                <div class="tdb-block-inner td-fix-index">
                                    <h1 class="tdb-title-text">Macro Magic: M4 Complete Guide</h1>
                                    <div class="tdb-title-line"></div>
                                </div>
                            </div>
                            <div class="td_block_wrap td_single_author_from_postmeta tdi_57 td-pb-border-top td_block_template_2" data-td-block-uid="tdi_57">    
                                <div class="tdb-block-inner td-fix-index">
                                    <div class="tdb-author-name-wrap">
                                        <span class="tdb-author-by">By</span>
                                        Jerry Peek                                    </div>
                                </div>
                            </div>
                            <div class="td_block_wrap tdb_single_date tdi_58 td-pb-border-top td_block_template_2 tdb-post-meta" data-td-block-uid="tdi_58">
                                <div class="tdb-block-inner td-fix-index">
                                    <time class="entry-date updated td-module-date" datetime="2019-05-09T22:39:00+00:00">May 9, 2019</time>
                                </div>
                            </div> <!-- ./block -->
                            <div class="td_block_wrap tdb_single_content tdi_59 td-pb-border-top td_block_template_2 td-post-content tagdiv-type" data-td-block-uid="tdi_59">
                                <div class="tdb-block-inner td-fix-index">
                                    <div class="form-custom-fields-container above-content"></div>
                                    <p>A&nbsp;<span class="i">macro processor</span>&nbsp;scans input text for defined symbols &mdash; the&nbsp;<span class="i">macros</span>&nbsp;&mdash; and replaces that text by other text, or possibly by other symbols. For instance, a macro processor can convert one language into another.</p>
<p>If you&#8217;re a&nbsp;<span class="i">C</span>&nbsp;programmer, you know&nbsp;<span class="i">cpp,</span>&nbsp;the C preprocessor, a simple macro processor.&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;is a powerful macro processor that&#8217;s been part of Unix for some 30 years, but it&#8217;s almost unknown &mdash; except for special purposes, such as generating the&nbsp;<span class="i">sendmail.cf</span>&nbsp;file. It&#8217;s worth knowing because you can do things with&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;that are hard to do any other way.</p>
<p>The GNU version of&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;has some extensions from the original&nbsp;<span class="i">V7</span>&nbsp;version. (You&#8217;ll see some of them.) As of this writing, the latest GNU version was&nbsp;<span class="i">1.4.2,</span>&nbsp;released in August 2004.&nbsp;<span class="i">Version 2.0</span>&nbsp;is under development.</p>
<p>While you won&#8217;t become an&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;wizard in three pages (or in six, as the discussion of&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>continues next month), but you can master the basics. So, let&#8217;s dig in.</p>
<p class="subhead">Simple Macro Processing</p>
<p>A simple way to do macro substitution is with tools like&nbsp;<span class="i">sed</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="i">cpp.</span>&nbsp;For instance, the command&nbsp;<span class="c">sed&#8217;s/XPRESIDENTX/President Bush/&#8217;</span>&nbsp;reads lines of text, changing every occurrence of&nbsp;<span class="c">XPRESIDENTX</span>&nbsp;to&nbsp;<span class="c">President Bush</span>.&nbsp;<span class="i">sed</span>&nbsp;can also test and branch, for some rudimentary decision-making.</p>
<p>As another example, here&#8217;s a C program with a&nbsp;<span class="i">cpp</span>&nbsp;macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">ABSDIFF()</span>&nbsp;that accepts two arguments,&nbsp;<span class="c">a</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">b</span>.</p>
<p><span class="pre">#define ABSDIFF(a, b) <br />((a)&gt;(b) ? (a)-(b) : (b)-(a))<br /></span></p>
<p>Given that definition,&nbsp;<span class="i">cpp</span>&nbsp;will replace the code&hellip;</p>
<p><span class="pre">diff = ABSDIFF(v1, v2);<br /></span></p>
<p>&hellip; with</p>
<p><span class="pre">diff = ((v1)&gt;(v2) ? (v1)-(v2) : (v2)-(v1));<br /></span></p>
<p><span class="c">v1</span>&nbsp;replaces&nbsp;<span class="c">a</span>&nbsp;everywhere, and&nbsp;<span class="c">v2</span>&nbsp;replace&nbsp;<span class="c">b</span>.&nbsp;<span class="c">ABSDIFF()</span>&nbsp;saves typing &mdash; and the chance for error.</p>
<p class="subhead">Introducing m4</p>
<p>Unlike&nbsp;<span class="i">sed</span>&nbsp;and other languages,&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;is designed specifically for macro processing.&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>manipulates files, performs arithmetic, has functions for handling strings, and can do much more.</p>
<p><span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;copies its input (from files or&nbsp;<span class="i">standard input</span>) to&nbsp;<span class="i">standard output.</span>&nbsp;It checks each token (a name, a quoted string, or any single character that&#8217;s not a part of either a name or a string) to see if it&#8217;s the name of a macro. If so, the token is replaced by the macro&#8217;s value, and then that text is pushed back onto the input to be rescanned. (If you&#8217;re new to&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>, this repeated scanning may surprise you, but it&#8217;s one key to&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;s power.) Quoting text, like&nbsp;<span class="c">`&nbsp;<span class="i">text</span>&#8216;</span>, prevents expansion. (See the section on &ldquo;Quoting.&rdquo;)</p>
<p><span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;comes with a number of predefined macros, or you can write your own macros by calling the&nbsp;<span class="c">define()</span>&nbsp;function. A macro can have multiple arguments&#8211; up to 9 in original&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>, and an unlimited number in GNU&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>. Macro arguments are substituted before the resulting text is rescanned.</p>
<p>Here&rsquo;s a simple example (saved in a file named&nbsp;<span class="i">foo.m4</span>):</p>
<p><span class="pre">one<br />define(`one&#8217;, `ONE&#8217;)dnl<br />one<br />define(`ONE&#8217;, `two&#8217;)dnl<br />one ONE oneONE<br />`one&#8217;<br /></span></p>
<p>The file defines two macros named&nbsp;<span class="i">one</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="i">ONE</span>. It also has four</p>
<p>lines of text. If you feed the file to&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;using&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 foo.m4</span>,&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;produces:</p>
<p><span class="pre">one<br />ONE<br />two two oneONE<br />one<br /></span></p>
<p>Here&#8217;s what&#8217;s happening:</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 1 of the input, which is simply the characters&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;and a newline, doesn&#8217;t match any macro (so far), so it&#8217;s copied to the output as-is.</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 2 defines a macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">one()</span>. (The opening parenthesis before the arguments must come just after&nbsp;<span class="c">define</span>&nbsp;with no whitespace between.) From this point on, any input string&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;will be replaced with&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE</span>. (The&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;is explained below.)</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 3, which is again the characters&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;and a newline, is affected by the just-defined macro&nbsp;<span class="c">one()</span>. So, the text&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;is converted to the text&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE</span>&nbsp;and a newline.</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 4 defines a new macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE()</span>. Macro names are case-sensitive.</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 5 has three space-separated tokens. The first two are&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE</span>. The first is converted to&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE</span>&nbsp;by the macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">one()</span>, then both are converted to&nbsp;<span class="c">two</span>&nbsp;by the macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">ONE()</span>. Rescanning doesn&rsquo;t find any additional matches (there&rsquo;s no macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">two()</span>), so the first two words are output as&nbsp;<span class="c">two two</span>. The rest of line 5 (a space,&nbsp;<span class="c">oneONE</span>, and a newline) doesn&rsquo;t match a macro so it&rsquo;s output as-is. In other words, a macro name is only recognized when it&rsquo;s surrounded by non-alphanumerics.</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">*</span>Line 6 contains the text&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>&nbsp;inside a pair of quotes, then a newline. (As you&#8217;ve seen, the opening quote is a backquote or grave accent; the closing quote is a single quote or acute accent.) Quoted text doesn&#8217;t match any macros, so it&#8217;s output as-is:&nbsp;<span class="c">one</span>. Next comes the final newline.</p>
<p>Input text is copied to the output as-is and that includes newlines. The built-in&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>function, which stands for &ldquo;delete to new line,&rdquo; reads and discards all characters up to and including the next newline. (One of its uses is to put comments into an&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;file.) Without&nbsp;<span class="i">dnl</span>, the newline after each of our calls to&nbsp;<span class="i">define</span>&nbsp;would be output as-is. We could demonstrate that by editing&nbsp;<span class="i">foo.m4</span>&nbsp;to remove the two&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;s. But, to stretch things a bit, let&rsquo;s use&nbsp;<span class="i">sed</span>&nbsp;to remove those two calls from the file and pipe the result to&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>:</p>
<p><span class="pre">$ sed &#8216;s/dnl//&#8217; foo.m4 | m4<br />one</p>
<p>ONE</p>
<p>two two oneONE<br />one<br /></span></p>
<p>If you compare this example to the previous one, you&#8217;ll see that there</p>
<p>are two extra newlines at the places where&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;used to be.</p>
<p>Let&rsquo;s summarize. You&rsquo;ve seen that input is read from the first character to the last. Macros affect input text only after they&rsquo;re defined. Input tokens are compared to macro names and, if they match, replaced by the macro&rsquo;s value. Any input modified by a macro is pushed back onto the input and is&nbsp;<span class="i">rescanned</span>&nbsp;for possible modification. Other text (that isn&rsquo;t modified by a macro) is passed to the output as-is.</p>
<p class="subhead">Quoting</p>
<p>Any text surrounded by&nbsp;<span class="c">`&#8217;</span>&nbsp;(a grave accent and an acute accent) isn&#8217;t expanded immediately. Whenever&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;evaluates something, it strips off one level of quotes. When you define a macro, you&#8217;ll often want to quote the arguments &mdash; but not always.&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing One</span>&nbsp;has a demo. It uses&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;interactively, typing text to its standard input.</p>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing One:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">Quoting demonstration</span></p>
<p><span class="pre">$ m4<br />define(A, 100)dnl<br />define(B, A)dnl<br />define(C, `A&#8217;)dnl<br />dumpdef(`A&#8217;, `B&#8217;, `C&#8217;)dnl<br />A: 100<br />B: 100<br />C: A<br />dumpdef(A, B, C)dnl<br />stdin:5: m4: Undefined name 100<br />stdin:5: m4: Undefined name 100<br />stdin:5: m4: Undefined name 100<br />A B C<br />100 100 100<br />CTRL-D<br />$<br /></span></p>
<p>The listing starts by defining three macros&nbsp;<span class="i">A</span>,&nbsp;<span class="i">B</span>, and&nbsp;<span class="i">C</span>.&nbsp;<span class="i">A</span>&nbsp;has the value 100. So does&nbsp;<span class="i">B</span>: because its argument&nbsp;<span class="c">A</span>&nbsp;isn&#8217;t quoted,&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;replaces&nbsp;<span class="c">A</span>&nbsp;with&nbsp;<span class="c">100</span>&nbsp;before assigning that value to&nbsp;<span class="i">B</span>. While defining&nbsp;<span class="i">C</span>, though, quoting the argument means that its value becomes literal&nbsp;<span class="c">A</span>.</p>
<p>You can see the values of macros by calling the built-in function&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef</span>&nbsp;with the names of the macros. As expected,&nbsp;<span class="i">A</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="i">B</span>&nbsp;have the value&nbsp;<span class="c">100</span>, but&nbsp;<span class="i">C</span>&nbsp;has&nbsp;<span class="c">A</span>.</p>
<p>In the second call to&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef</span>, the names are not quoted, so each name is expanded to&nbsp;<span class="c">100</span><span class="i">before</span>&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef</span>&nbsp;sees them. That explains the error messages, because there&rsquo;s no macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">100</span>. In the same way, if we simply enter the macro names, the three tokens are scanned repeatedly, and they all end up as&nbsp;<span class="c">100</span>.</p>
<p>You can change the quoting characters at any time by calling&nbsp;<span class="c">changequote</span>. For instance, in text containing lots of quote marks, you could call&nbsp;<span class="c">changequote({,})dnl</span>&nbsp;to change the quoting characters to curly braces. To restore the defaults, simply call&nbsp;<span class="c">changequote</span>&nbsp;with no arguments.</p>
<p>In general, for safety, it&rsquo;s a good idea to quote all input text that isn&rsquo;t a macro call. This avoids&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;interpreting a literal word as a call to a macro. Another way to avoid this problem is by using the GNU&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;option&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;&ndash;prefix-builtins</span>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;P</span>. It changes all built-in macro names to be prefixed by&nbsp;<span class="c">m4_</span>. (The option doesn&rsquo;t affect user-defined macros.) So, under this option, you&rsquo;d write&nbsp;<span class="c">m4_dnl</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">m4_define</span>&nbsp;instead of&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">define</span>, respectively.</p>
<p>Keep quoting and rescanning in mind as you use&nbsp;<span class="i">m4.</span>&nbsp;Not to be tedious, but remember that&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;<span class="i">does</span>&nbsp;rescan its input. For some in-depth tips, see &ldquo;Web Paging: Tips and Hints on&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>Quoting&rdquo; by R.K. Owen, Ph.D., at&nbsp;<a class="story_link" href="http://owen.sj.ca.us/rkowen/howto/webpaging/m4tipsquote.html">http://owen.sj.ca.us/rkowen/howto/webpaging/m4tipsquote.html</a>.</p>
<p class="subhead">Decisions and Math</p>
<p><span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;can do arithmetic with its built-in functions&nbsp;<span class="c">eval</span>,&nbsp;<span class="c">incr</span>, and&nbsp;<span class="c">decr</span>.&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;doesn&#8217;t support loops directly, but you can combine recursion and the decision macro&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>&nbsp;to write loops.</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s start with an example adapted from the file&nbsp;<span class="i">/usr/share/doc/m4/examples/debug.m4</span>(on a Debian system). It defines the macro&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown()</span>. Evaluating the macro with an argument of 5 &mdash; as in&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown(5)</span>&nbsp;&mdash; outputs the text&nbsp;<span class="c">5, 4, 3, 2, 1, 0, Liftoff!</span>.</p>
<p><span class="pre">$ cat countdown.m4<br />define(`countdown&#8217;, `$1, ifelse(eval($1 &gt; 0),<br />1, `countdown(decr($1))&#8217;, `Liftoff!&#8217;)&#8217;)dnl<br />countdown(5)<br />$ m4 countdown.m4<br />5, 4, 3, 2, 1, 0, Liftoff!<br /></span></p>
<p>The&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown()</span>&nbsp;macro has a single argument. It&#8217;s broken across two</p>
<p><span class="enumeration">lines.</span>That&#8217;s fine in&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;because macro arguments are delimited by</p>
<p>parentheses which don&#8217;t have to be on the same line. Here&#8217;s the</p>
<p>argument without its surrounding quotes:</p>
<p><span class="pre">$1, ifelse(eval($1 &gt; 0), 1,<br />`countdown(decr($1))&#8217;, `Liftoff!&#8217;)<br />)<br /></span></p>
<p><span class="c">$1</span>&nbsp;expands to the macro&rsquo;s first argument. When&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;evaluates that</p>
<p><span class="c">countdown</span>&nbsp;macro with an argument of&nbsp;<span class="c">5</span>, the result is:</p>
<p><span class="pre">5, ifelse(eval(5 &gt; 0), 1,<br />`countdown(decr(5))&#8217;, `Liftoff!&#8217;)<br /></span></p>
<p>The leading &ldquo;&nbsp;<span class="c">5,</span>&nbsp;&ldquo; is plain text that&rsquo;s output as-is as the first number in the countdown. The rest of the argument is a call to&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>.&nbsp;<span class="c">Ifelse</span>&nbsp;compares its first two arguments. If they&rsquo;re equal, the third argument is evaluated; otherwise, the (optional) fourth argument is evaluated.</p>
<p>Here, the first argument to&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>,&nbsp;<span class="c">eval(5&gt; 0)</span>, evaluates as 1 (logical&#8221; true&#8221;) if the test is true (if 5 is greater than 0). So the first two arguments are equal, and&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;evaluates&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown(decr(5))</span>. This starts the recursion by calling&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown(4)</span>.</p>
<p>Once we reach the base condition of&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown(0)</span>, the test&nbsp;<span class="c">eval(0&gt; 0)</span>&nbsp;fails and the&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>call evaluates&nbsp;<span class="c">`Liftoff!&#8217;</span>. (If recursion is new to you, you can read about it in books on computer science and programming techniques.)</p>
<p>Note that, with more than four arguments,&nbsp;<span class="i">ifelse</span>&nbsp;can work like a&nbsp;<span class="i">case</span>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<span class="i">switch</span>&nbsp;in other languages. For instance, in&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse(a,b,c,d,e,f,g)</span>, if&nbsp;<span class="c">a</span>&nbsp;matches&nbsp;<span class="c">b</span>, then&nbsp;<span class="c">c</span>; else if&nbsp;<span class="c">d</span>&nbsp;matches&nbsp;<span class="c">e</span>then&nbsp;<span class="c">f</span>; else&nbsp;<span class="c">g</span>.</p>
<p>The&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;<span class="i">info</span>&nbsp;file shows more looping and decision techniques, including a macro named&nbsp;<span class="c">forloop()</span>&nbsp;that implements a nestable for-loop.</p>
<p>This section showed some basic math operations. (The&nbsp;<span class="i">info</span>&nbsp;file shows more.) You&rsquo;ve seen that you can quote a single macro argument that contains a completely separate string (in this case, a string that prints a number, then runs&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>&nbsp;to do some more work). This one-line example (broken onto two lines here) is a good hint of&nbsp;<span class="i">m4&rsquo;</span>&nbsp;s power. It&rsquo;s a mimimalist language, for sure, and you&rsquo;d be right to complain about its tricky evaluation in a global environment, leaving lots of room for trouble if you aren&rsquo;t careful. But you might find this expressive little language to be challenging enough that it&rsquo;s addictive.</p>
<p class="subhead">Building Web Pages</p>
<p>Let&rsquo;s wrap up this&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;introduction with a typical use: feeding an input file to a set of macros to generate an output file. Here, the macro file&nbsp;<span class="i">html.m4</span>&nbsp;defines three macros:&nbsp;<span class="c">_startpage()</span>,&nbsp;<span class="c">_ul()</span>, and&nbsp;<span class="c">_endpage()</span>. (The names start with underscore characters to help prevent false matches with non-macro text. For instance,&nbsp;<span class="c">_ul()</span>&nbsp;won&rsquo;t match the HTML tag&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;ul</span>&amp; gt;.) The&nbsp;<span class="c">_startpage()</span>&nbsp;macro accepts one argument: the page title, which is also copied into a level-1 heading that appears at the start of the page. The&nbsp;<span class="c">_ul()</span>&nbsp;macro makes an HTML unordered list. Its arguments (an unlimited number) become the list items. And&nbsp;<span class="c">_endpage()</span>&nbsp;makes the closing HTML text, including a &ldquo;last change&rdquo; date taken from the Linux&nbsp;<span class="i">date</span>&nbsp;utility.</p>
<p><span class="i">Listing Two</span>&nbsp;shows the input file, and&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Three</span>&nbsp;is the HTML output. The&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;macros that do all the work are shown in&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Four.</span>&nbsp;(Both the input file and the macros are available online at</p>
<p><a class="story_link" href="http://www.linux-mag.com/downloads/2005-02/power">http://www.linux-mag.com/downloads/2005-02/power</a>.)</p>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Two:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption"><span class="i">webpage.m4h,</span>&nbsp;an&#8221; unexpanded&#8221; web page</span></p>
<p><span class="pre">_startpage(`Sample List&#8217;)<br />_ul(`First item&#8217;, `Second item&#8217;,<br />`Third item, longer than the first two&#8217;)<br />_endpage<br /></span></p>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Three:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">An&nbsp;<span class="i">m4-</span>&nbsp;generated web page</span></p>
<p><span class="pre">$ m4 html.m4 webpage.m4h &gt; list.html<br />$ cat list.html<br />&lt;html&gt;<br />&lt;head&gt;<br />&lt;title&gt;Sample List&lt;/title&gt;<br />&lt;/head&gt;<br />&lt;body&gt;<br />&lt;h1&gt;Sample List&lt;/h1&gt;<br />&lt;ul&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;First item&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;Second item&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;Third item, longer than the first two&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;/ul&gt;</p>
<p>&lt;p&gt;Last change: Fri Jan 14 15:32:06 MST 2005<br />&lt;/p&gt;<br />&lt;/body&gt;<br />&lt;/html&gt;<br /></span></p>
<p>In&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Four,</span>&nbsp;both&nbsp;<span class="c">_startpage()</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">_endpage()</span>&nbsp;are straightforward. The&nbsp;<span class="c">esyscmd</span>macro is one of the many&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;macros we haven&rsquo;t covered &mdash; it runs a Linux command line, then uses the command&rsquo;s output as input to&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>. The&nbsp;<span class="c">_ul()</span>&nbsp;macro outputs opening and closing HTML&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;ul&gt;</span>&nbsp;tags, passing its arguments to the&nbsp;<span class="c">_listitems()</span>&nbsp;macro via&nbsp;<span class="c">$@</span>, which expands into the quoted list of arguments.</p>
<p><span class="c">_listitems()</span>&nbsp;is similar to the&nbsp;<span class="c">countdown()</span>&nbsp;macro shown earlier:&nbsp;<span class="c">_listitems()</span>&nbsp;makes a recursive loop. At the base condition (the end of recursion), when&nbsp;<span class="c">$#</span>&nbsp;(the number of arguments) is 0, the empty third argument means that&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>&nbsp;does nothing. Or, if there&rsquo;s one argument (<span class="c">$#</span>&nbsp;is 1),&nbsp;<span class="c">ifelse</span>&nbsp;simply outputs the last list item inside a pair of&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;li&gt;</span>&nbsp;tags. Otherwise, there&rsquo;s more than one argument, so the macro starts by outputting the first argument inside&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;li&gt;</span>&nbsp;tags, then calls&nbsp;<span class="c">_listitems()</span>&nbsp;recursively to output the other list items. The argument to the recursive call is&nbsp;<span class="c">shift($@)</span>. The&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;<span class="c">shift</span>&nbsp;macro returns its list of arguments without its first argument &mdash; which, here, is all of the arguments we haven&rsquo;t processed yet.</p>
<p>Notice the nested quoting: some of the arguments inside the (quoted) definition of&nbsp;<span class="c">_listitems()</span>&nbsp;are quoted themselves. This delays interpretation until the macro is called. (m4 tracing, which we&rsquo;ll cover next month, can help you see what&rsquo;s happening.)</p>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Four:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption"><span class="i">html.m4,</span>&nbsp;macros to generate an HTML page from&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Two</span></span></p>
<p><span class="pre">define(`_startpage&#8217;, `<br />&lt;head&gt;<br />&lt;title&gt;$1&lt;/title&gt;<br />&lt;/head&gt;<br />&lt;body&gt;<br />&lt;h1&gt;$1&lt;/h1&gt;&#8217;)dnl<br />dnl<br />define(`_endpage&#8217;, `<br />&lt;p&gt;Last change: esyscmd(date)&lt;/p&gt;<br />&lt;/body&gt;<br />&lt;/html&gt;&#8217;)dnl<br />dnl<br />define(`_listitems&#8217;, `ifelse($#, 0, ,<br />$#, 1, `&lt;li&gt;$1&lt;/li&gt;&#8217;,<br />`&lt;li&gt;$1&lt;/li&gt;<br />_listitems(shift($@))&#8217;)&#8217;)dnl<br />define(`_ul&#8217;, `&lt;ul&gt;<br />_listitems($@)<br />&lt;/ul&gt;&#8217;)dnl<br /></span></p>
<div>
<div class="prose">This month, let&rsquo;s dig deeper into m4 and look at included files,&nbsp;<span class="i">diversions,</span>&nbsp;<span class="i">frozen files,</span>&nbsp;and debugging and tracing. Along the way, we&rsquo;ll see some of the rough edges of m4&rsquo;s minimalist language and explore workarounds. Before we start, though, here&rsquo;s a warning from the GNU m4&nbsp;<span class="i">info</span>&nbsp;page:</div>
<div class="quoteblock">
<div class="prose">Some people[ find] m4 to be fairly addictive. They first use m4 for simple problems, then take bigger and bigger challenges, learning how to write complex m4 sets of macros along the way. Once really addicted, users pursue writing of sophisticated m4 applications even to solve simple problems, devoting more time debugging their m4 scripts than doing real work. Beware that m4 may be dangerous for the health of compulsive programmers.</div>
</div>
<div class="prose">So take a deep breath&hellip; Good. Now let&rsquo;s dig in again!</div>
<p class="subhead">Included Files</p>
<div class="prose">m4&rsquo;s built-in&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;macro takes m4&rsquo;s input from a named file until the end of that file, when the previous input resumes.&nbsp;<span class="c">sinclude()</span>&nbsp;works like&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;except that it won&rsquo;t complain if the included file doesn&rsquo;t exist.</div>
<div class="prose">If an included file isn&rsquo;t in the current directory, GNU m4 searches the directories specified with the&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;I</span>command-line option, followed by any directories in the colon-separated&nbsp;<span class="c">M4PATH</span>&nbsp;environment variable.</div>
<div class="prose">Including files is often used to read in other m4 code, but can also be used to read plain text files. However, if you&rsquo;re reading plain text files, watch out for files that contain text that can confuse m4, such as quotes, commas, and parentheses. One way to work around that problem and read the contents of a random file is by using&nbsp;<span class="c">changequote()</span>&nbsp;to temporarily override the quoting characters and also replacing&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;with&nbsp;<span class="c">esyscmd()</span>, which filters the file through a Linux utility like&nbsp;<span class="i">tr</span>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<span class="i">sed.</span></div>
<div class="prose"><span class="i">Listing One</span>&nbsp;has a contrived example that shows one way to read&nbsp;<span class="i">/etc/hosts,</span>&nbsp;replacing parentheses with square brackets and commas with dashes.</div>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing One:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">A filtering version of the&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;macro</span></div>
<pre>% cat readfile.m4<br />dnl readfile: display file named on<br />dnl command line in &ndash;Dfile=<name><br />dnl converting () to [] and , to -<br />dnl<br />file `file on &rsquo;esyscmd(`hostname&rsquo;)<br />changequote({,})dnl<br />esyscmd({tr &rsquo;(),&rsquo; &rsquo;[]-&rsquo; &lt;} file)dnl<br />
That&rsquo;s all.<br />changequote<br />% cat /etc/hosts<br />127.0.0.1       localhost<br />216.123.4.56    foo.bar.com        foo<br />
# Following lines are for `IPv6&rsquo;<br /># (added automatically, we hope)<br />::1     ip6-localhost ip6-loopback<br />&hellip;<br />
% m4 &ndash;Dfile=/etc/hosts readfile.m4<br />/etc/hosts file on foo<br />
127.0.0.1       localhost<br />234.123.4.56    foo.bar.com        foo<br />
# Following lines are for `IPv6&rsquo;<br /># [added automatically- we hope]<br />::1     ip6-localhost ip6-loopback<br />&hellip;<br />
That&rsquo;s all.<br /></name></pre>
</div>
<div class="prose">The option&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;D</span>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;&ndash;define</span>&nbsp;lets you define a macro from the command line, before any input files are read. (Later, we&rsquo;ll see an cleaner way to read text from arbitrary files with GNU m4&rsquo;s&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>.)</div>
<p class="subhead">Diversions: An Overview</p>
<div class="prose">Normally, all output is written directly to m4&rsquo;s&nbsp;<span class="i">standard output.</span>&nbsp;But you can use the&nbsp;<span class="c">divert()</span>&nbsp;macro to collect output into temporary storage places. This is one of m4&rsquo;s handiest features.</div>
<div class="prose">The argument to&nbsp;<span class="c">divert()</span>&nbsp;is typically a stream number, the ID of the diversion that should get the output from now on.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>Diversion 0 is the default. Text written to diversion 0 goes to m4&rsquo;s standard output. If you&rsquo;ve been diverting text to another stream, you can call&nbsp;<span class="c">divert(0)</span>&nbsp;or just&nbsp;<span class="c">divert</span>&nbsp;to resume normal output.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>Text written to diversions 1, 2, and so on is held until m4 exits or until you call&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>. (More about that in a moment.)</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>Any text written to diversion&nbsp;<span class="i">-1</span>&nbsp;isn&rsquo;t emitted. Instead, diversion- 1 is &ldquo;nowhere,&rdquo; like the Linux pseudo-file&nbsp;<span class="i">/dev/null.</span>&nbsp;It&rsquo;s often used to comment code and to define macros without using the pesky&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;macro at the ends of lines.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">divnum</span>&nbsp;macro outputs the current diversion number.</div>
<div class="prose">Standard m4 supports diversions- 1 through 9, while GNU m4 can handle a essentially unlimited number of diversions. The latter version of m4 holds diverted text in memory until it runs out of memory and then moves the largest chunks of data to temporary files. (So, in theory, the number of diversions in GNU m4 is limited to the number of available file descriptors.)</div>
<div class="prose">All diversions 1, 2,&#8230;, are output at the end of processing in ascending order of stream number. To output diverted text sooner, simply call&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>&nbsp;with the stream number.&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>&nbsp;outputs text from a diversion and then empties the diversion. So, immediately calling&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>&nbsp;again on the same diversion outputs nothing.</div>
<div class="prose">&ldquo;Undiverted&rdquo; text is output to&nbsp;<span class="i">the current diversion,</span>&nbsp;which isn&rsquo;t always the standard output! You can use this to move text from one diversion to another. Output from a diversion is&nbsp;<span class="i">not</span>&nbsp;rescanned for macros.</div>
<p class="subhead">Diverse Diversions</p>
<div class="prose">Before looking at the more-obvious uses of numbered diversions, let&rsquo;s look at a few surprising ones.</div>
<div class="prose">As was mentioned, diversion- 1 discards output. One of the most irritating types of m4 output is the newline chanacters after macro definitions. You can stop them by calling&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;after each&nbsp;<span class="c">define</span>, but you can also stop them by defining macros after a call to&nbsp;<span class="c">divert(-1)</span>.</div>
<div class="prose">Here are two examples. This first example,&nbsp;<span class="i">nl,</span>&nbsp;doesn&rsquo;t suppress the newline from&nbsp;<span class="i">define&hellip;</span></div>
<pre>`The result is:&rsquo;<br />define(`name&rsquo;, `value&rsquo;)<br />name</pre>
<div class="prose">&hellip; but the next example,&nbsp;<span class="i">nonl,</span>&nbsp;does, by defining the macro inside a diversion:</div>
<pre>`The result is:&rsquo;<br />divert(-1)<br />define(`name&rsquo;, `value&rsquo;)<br />divert(0)dnl<br />name</pre>
<div class="prose">Let&rsquo;s compare the&nbsp;<span class="i">nl</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="i">nonl</span>&nbsp;versions.</div>
<pre>$ m4 nl<br />The result is:<br />
value<br />$ m4 nonl<br />The result is:<br />value</pre>
<div class="prose">The second&nbsp;<span class="c">divert()</span>&nbsp;ends with&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>, which eats the the following newline. Adding the argument&nbsp;<span class="c">(0)</span>, which is actually the default, lets you write&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;without a space before it (which would otherwise be output). You can use&nbsp;<span class="c">divert`&rsquo;dnl</span>&nbsp;instead, because an empty quoted string (<span class="c">`&rsquo;</span>) is another way to separate the&nbsp;<span class="c">divert</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;macro calls.</div>
<div class="prose">Of course, that trick is more reasonably done around a group of several&nbsp;<span class="c">define</span>&nbsp;s. You can also write comments inside the same kind of diversion. This is an easy way to write blocks of comments without putting&nbsp;<span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;at the start of each line. Just remember that macros&nbsp;<span class="i">are</span>&nbsp;recognized inside the diversion (even though they don&rsquo;t make output). So, the following code increments&nbsp;<span class="c">i</span>&nbsp;<span class="i">twice:</span></div>
<pre>divert(-1)<br />Now we run define(`i&rsquo;, incr(i)):<br />define(`i&rsquo;, incr(i))<br />divert`&rsquo;dnl</pre>
<div class="prose"><span class="c">dnl</span>&nbsp;can start comments, and that works on even the oldest versions of m4. Generally,&nbsp;<span class="c">#</span>&nbsp;is also a comment character. If you put it at the start of the comment above, as in&nbsp;<span class="c">#Now&hellip;</span>, then&nbsp;<span class="i">i</span>&nbsp;won&rsquo;t be incremented.</div>
<div class="prose">Before seeing the &ldquo;obvious&rdquo; uses of diversions, here&rsquo;s one last item from the bag of diversion tricks. GNU m4 lets you output a file&rsquo;s contents by calling&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert()</span>&nbsp;instead of&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>. The advantage is that, like undiverting a diversion, &ldquo;undiverting&rdquo; a file doesn&rsquo;t scan the file&rsquo;s contents for macros. This lets you avoid the really ugly workaround showed in&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing One.</span>&nbsp;With GNU m4, you could have written simply:</div>
<pre>undivert(`/etc/hosts&rsquo;)</pre>
<p class="subhead">Diversions as Diversions</p>
<div class="prose">The previous section showed some offbeat uses of&nbsp;<span class="c">divert()</span>. Now let&rsquo;s see a more obvious use: splitting output into parts and reassembling those parts in a different order.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="i">Listing Two, Three,</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="i">Four</span>&nbsp;show a&nbsp;<span class="i">HTML</span>&nbsp;generator that outputs the text of each top-level heading in two places: in a table of contents at the start of the web page, and again, later, in the body of the web page. The table of contents includes links to the actual headings later in the document, which will have an anchor (an HTML&nbsp;<span class="c">id</span>).</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="i">Listing Two</span>&nbsp;has the file,&nbsp;<span class="i">htmltext.m4,</span>&nbsp;with the macro calls.&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Three</span>&nbsp;shows the HTML output from the macros (which omits the blank lines, because HTML parsers ignore them).&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Four</span>&nbsp;shows the macros, which call&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;to bring in the&nbsp;<span class="i">htmltext.m4</span>&nbsp;file at the proper place. (Blank lines have been added to the macros to make the start and end of each macro more obvious.)</div>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Two:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">m4 macro calls: the&nbsp;<span class="i">htmltext.m4</span>&nbsp;file</span></div>
<pre>_h1(`First heading&rsquo;)<br />_p(`The first paragraph.&rsquo;)<br />_h1(`Second heading&rsquo;)<br />_p(`The second paragraph.<br />Yadda yadda yadda&rsquo;)<br />_h1(`Third heading&rsquo;)<br />_p(`The third paragraph.&rsquo;)</pre>
</div>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Three:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">HTML output from the&nbsp;<span class="i">htmltext.m4</span>&nbsp;file</span></div>
<pre>&lt;strong&gt;Table of contents:&lt;/strong&gt;<br />&lt;ol&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="#H1_1"&gt;First heading&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="#H1_2"&gt;Second heading&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="#H1_3"&gt;Third heading&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;<br />&lt;/ol&gt;<br />&lt;h1 id="H1_1"&gt;First heading&lt;/h1&gt;<br />&lt;p&gt;<br />The first paragraph.<br />&lt;/p&gt;<br />&lt;h1 id="H1_2"&gt;Second heading&lt;/h1&gt;<br />&lt;p&gt;<br />The second paragraph.<br />Yadda yadda yadda<br />&lt;/p&gt;<br />&lt;h1 id="H1_3"&gt;Third heading&lt;/h1&gt;<br />&lt;p&gt;<br />The third paragraph.<br />&lt;/p&gt;</pre>
</div>
<div class="listing">
<div class="sidebartitle"><span class="callout">Listing Four:&nbsp;</span><span class="caption">The&nbsp;<span class="i">m4</span>&nbsp;code that makes the HTML in&nbsp;<span class="i">Listing Three</span></span></div>
<pre>define(`_h1count&rsquo;, 0)<br />
define(`_h1&rsquo;, `divert(9)<br />define(`_h1count&rsquo;, incr(_h1count))<br />&lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="`#&rsquo;H1`_&rsquo;_h1count"&gt;$1&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;<br />divert(1)<br />&lt;h1 id="H1`_&rsquo;_h1count"&gt;$1&lt;/h1&gt;<br />divert&rsquo;)<br />
define(`_p&rsquo;, `divert(1)<br />&lt;p&gt;<br />$1<br />&lt;/p&gt;<br />divert&rsquo;)<br />
include(`htmltext.m4&rsquo;)<br />
&lt;strong&gt;Table of contents:&lt;/strong&gt;<br />&lt;ol&gt;<br />undivert(9)<br />&lt;/ol&gt;<br />undivert(1)</pre>
</div>
<div class="prose">Let&rsquo;s look at the code in Listing Four.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">_h1count</span>&nbsp;macro sets the number used at the end of each HTML&nbsp;<span class="i">id</span>. It&rsquo;s incremented by a&nbsp;<span class="i">define</span>&nbsp;call inside the&nbsp;<span class="i">_h1</span>&nbsp;macro.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">_h1</span>&nbsp;(heading level 1) macro starts by calling&nbsp;<span class="c">divert(9)</span>. The code used diversion 9 to store the HTML for the table of contents. After incrementing&nbsp;<span class="c">_h1count</span>, the macro outputs a list item surrounded by&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;li&gt;</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;/li&gt;</span>tags. (The&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;ol&gt;</span>&nbsp;tags come later: when the code undiverts diversion 9.) Notice that the&nbsp;<span class="c">#</span>&nbsp;is quoted to keep it from being treated as an m4 comment character. In the same way, the underscore is quoted (<span class="c">`_&rsquo;</span>), since it&rsquo;s used as part of the HTML&nbsp;<span class="c">id</span>&nbsp;string (for instance,&nbsp;<span class="c">href=&#8221;#H1_2&#8243;</span>).</div>
<div class="prose">A final call to&nbsp;<span class="i">divert</span>&nbsp;switches output back to the normal diversion 0, which is m4&rsquo;s standard output.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">_p</span>&nbsp;(paragraph) macro is straightforward. It stores a pair of&nbsp;<span class="c">&lt;p&gt;</span>&nbsp;tags with the first macro argument in-between in diversion 1.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>A call to&nbsp;<span class="c">include()</span>&nbsp;brings in the file&nbsp;<span class="i">htmltext.m4</span>&nbsp;(<span class="i">Listing Two</span>). This could have done this in several other ways, on the m4 command line, for instance.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>Finally, the call&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert(9)</span>&nbsp;outputs the table of contents surrounded by a pair of ordered-list tags, followed by the headers and paragraphs from&nbsp;<span class="c">undivert(1)</span>.</div>
<div class="prose">This example shows one use of diversions: to output text in more than one way. Another common use &mdash; in&nbsp;<span class="i">sendmail</span>, for instance &mdash; is gathering various text into &ldquo;bunches&rdquo; by its type or purpose.</div>
<p class="subhead">Frozen Files</p>
<div class="prose">Large m4 applications can take time to load. GNU m4 supports a feature called frozen files that speeds up loading of common base files. For instance, if your common definitions are stored in a file named&nbsp;<span class="i">common.m4,</span>you can pre-process that file to create a frozen file containing the m4 state information:</div>
<pre>$ m4 &ndash;F common.m4f common.m4</pre>
<div class="prose">Then, instead of using&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 common.m4</span>, you use&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 &ndash;R common.m4f</span>&nbsp;for faster access to the&nbsp;<span class="i">common</span>definitions.</div>
<div class="prose">Frozen files work in a majority of cases, but there are gotchas. Be sure to read the m4&nbsp;<span class="i">info</span>&nbsp;file (type&nbsp;<span class="c">info m4</span>) before you use this feature.</div>
<p class="subhead">Debugging and Tracing</p>
<div class="prose">m4&rsquo;s recursion and quoting can make debugging a challenge. A thorough understanding of m4 helps, of course, and the techniques shown in the next section are worth studying. Here are some built-in debugging techniques:</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>To see a macro definition, use&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef()</span>, which was covered last month.&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef()</span>&nbsp;shows you what&rsquo;s left after the initial layer of quoting is stripped off of a macro definition and any substitutions are made.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">traceon()</span>&nbsp;macro traces the execution of the macros you name as arguments, or, without a list of macros, it traces all macros. The trace output shows the depth of expansion, which is typically&nbsp;<span class="c">1</span>, but can be greater if a macro contains macro calls. Use&nbsp;<span class="c">traceoff</span>&nbsp;to stop tracing.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>The&nbsp;<span class="c">debugmode()</span>&nbsp;macro gives you a lot of control over debugging output. It accepts a string of flags, which are described in the m4 info file. You can also specify debugging flags on the command line with&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;d</span>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;&ndash;debug</span>. These flags also affect the output of&nbsp;<span class="c">dumpdef()</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">traceon()</span>.</div>
<p class="subhead">More about m4</p>
<div class="prose">Last month and this, you&rsquo;ve seen some highlights of m4. If you have the</div>
<div class="prose">GNU version of m4, its info page (<span class="c">info m4</span>) is a good place to learn more.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">R.</span>K. Owen&rsquo;s quoting page (<a class="story_link" href="http://owen.sj.ca.us/rkowen/howto/webpaging/m4tipsquote.html">http://owen.sj.ca.us/rkowen/howto/webpaging/m4tipsquote.html</a>) has lots of tips about &mdash; what else &mdash; quoting in m4. His site also has other m4 information and examples.</div>
<div class="prose">Ken Turner&rsquo;s technical report &ldquo;CSM-126: Exploiting the m4 Macro Language,&rdquo; available from&nbsp;<a class="story_link" href="http://www.cs.stir.ac.uk/research/publications/techreps/previous.html">http://www.cs.stir.ac.uk/research/publications/techreps/previous.html</a>, shows a number of m4 techniques.</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>A Google search for&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 macro</span>&nbsp;turns up a variety of references.</div>
<div class="prose">To find example code, try a search with an m4-specific macro name, like&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 dnl</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="c">m4 divert &ndash;motorway</span>. (In Google, the</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="c">&ndash;motorway</span>&nbsp;avoids matches of the British road named the M4. You</div>
<div class="prose">can also add&nbsp;<span class="c">&ndash;sendmail</span>&nbsp;to skip&nbsp;<span class="i">sendmail</span>&#8211; specific information.)</div>
<div class="prose"><span class="enumeration">*</span>Mailing lists about m4 are at&nbsp;<a class="story_link" href="http://savannah.gnu.org/mail/?group">http://savannah.gnu.org/mail/?group</a>= m4.</div>
<div class="prose">Happy m4 hacking!</div>
</div>
<p class="bio">Jerry Peek is a freelance writer and instructor who has used Unix and Linux for over 20 years. He&rsquo;s happy to hear from readers; see&nbsp;<a class="story_link" href="http://www.jpeek.com/contact.html">http://www.jpeek.com/contact.html</a>.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
